The Conquests and the Consensus of the Companions
Concludes with conquests and a hadith as proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how 'Uthman's conquests and a hadith from Ibn 'Umar support his superiority.

Core Idea
The conquests in North Africa and the Companions' consensus confirm 'Uthman's high rank.

The author presents two final arguments: the military expansions during 'Uthman's caliphate, which he considers better than personal combat, and the testimony of Ibn 'Umar that during the Prophet's time the Companions held the order: Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then 'Uthman. This hadith strongly supports the Maturidi view.

Guided Path
1
Read about the conquests
Note that the author mentions conquests in the Maghreb (North Africa) that occurred during 'Uthman's reign through his governors.
2
Understand the comparison
The author argues that these conquests are better for 'Uthman than killing thousands in battle, implying his leadership brought greater good.
3
Examine the hadith of Ibn 'Umar
Read the narration from Abu Dawud. It states that the Companions used to say: 'No one is equal to Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then 'Uthman.' This reflects the early consensus.

The Hanafi Position on Uthman's Virtue
Nasafi states the Hanafi position on Uthman's virtue and provides hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi-Maturidi position on the ranking of the first three caliphs and the evidence used to support the preference of Uthman over Ali.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that Uthman is the third best after Abu Bakr and Umar.

This lesson presents the Hanafi doctrinal position on the virtue of Uthman ibn Affan. Imam Nasafi not only states the school's view but also defends it against objections and provides hadith evidence from the Companions' consensus. By studying this passage, you will see how the Maturidi school combines rational argument and transmitted proof to settle a sensitive theological issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analysis. Pay attention to the opening doctrine and the flow of argument.
This will give you an overview.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and dissenting views
Locate where Nasafi states the school's stance and what other scholars said. Understand the context of the debate.
3
Analyze the defense of Uthman's bravery
Nasafi addresses the potential issue of Uthman's withdrawal at Uhud. See how he redefines bravery and uses the siege as evidence.
This shows a kalam method of reasoning.
4
Examine the hadith proofs
Read the three hadiths from Abu Dawud. Notice that they establish a consensus among the Companions about the order of virtue.
These transmitted proofs are central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر مذهب أصحابنا القول بتفضيل عثمان رضي الله عنه بعد أبي بكر وعمر، وذهب الحسين بن الفضل البجلي ومحمد بن إسحاق بن خزيمة من أهل الحديث إلى تفضيل علي على عثمان، وتوقف أبو العباس القلانسي في ذلك وكان يرى إمامة المفضول جائزة، ومناقبه كثيرة، وبذله الأموال في نصرة دين الله وإقامة النبي عليه السلام إحدى يديه مقام يد عثمان في بيعة الرضوان معروفة، والأخبار بأن الملائكة تستحي منه مشهورة، ثم انحيازه في حرب أحد لا يدل على جبنه إذ قد يتفق ذلك أحيانا للبطل الكمي لعارض أمر خفي، ثم الشجاعة ليست إلا التهاون بالموت وعدم المبالاة من التلف والهلاك، وقد ظهر ذلك منه يوم الدار من منعة ناصريه عن القتال، وتعليق عتق عبيده بإلقاء السلاح على وجه لا تسمح به نفس أشهر خليقة الله بالشجاعة. ثم ما ظهر من الفتوح في أيامه من قبل المغرب على يدي عماله وأصحاب جيوشه، وذلك خير له من قتل ألوف من أبناء الحرب فيما بين الطعن والضرب، والذي يؤيد ما ذهبنا إليه ما روى أبو داود السجستاني في كتاب السنن بإسناده رضي الله عنهما عن ابن عمر أنه قال: كنا نقول في زمن النبي عليه السلام: لا يعدل بأبي بكر أحد ثم عمر ثم عثمان. وروى أيضا عن ابن عمر أنه قال: كنا نقول ورسول الله عليه السلام حي: أفضل أمة النبي عليه السلام بعده أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان. وروى أبو داود أيضا عن محمد بن الحنفية: قال: قلت يا أبي أي الناس خير بعد رسول الله؟ قال: أبو بكر، قلت: ثم من؟ قال: عمر، قال: ثم خشيت أن أقول: ثم من، فيقول: عثمان، فقلت أنت يا أبت؟ قال ما أنا إلا رجل من المسلمين. فثبت بهذه الأحاديث ما ادعينا من ترتيب الفضيلة، والله الموفق.
The apparent doctrine of our companions is the statement of preferring Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, after Abu Bakr and Umar. Al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzaymah from the people of hadith went to preferring Ali over Uthman, and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi hesitated in that and he held that the imamate of the inferior is permissible. His merits are many, and his spending of wealth in support of the religion of Allah and the Prophet's (peace be upon him) placing one of his hands in place of Uthman's hand at the pledge of Ridwan is well-known, and the reports that the angels are shy of him are famous. Then his withdrawal in the battle of Uhud does not indicate cowardice, for that may sometimes happen to the brave hero due to some hidden cause. Then bravery is nothing but recklessness with death and lack of concern for destruction and perdition, and that was manifest from him on the day of the house (i.e., siege) in the protection of his supporters from fighting, and his suspending the freeing of his slaves upon laying down arms in a manner that the soul of the most famous creature of Allah for bravery would not permit. Then what appeared of conquests in his days from the direction of the Maghrib at the hands of his governors and army commanders, and that is better for him than killing thousands of the sons of war amidst thrusting and striking. And that which supports what we have gone to is what Abu Dawud al-Sijistani narrated in the book of Sunan with his chain, may Allah be pleased with both of them, from Ibn Umar that he said: We used to say in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him): No one is equal to Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. And he also narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: We used to say while the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was alive: The best of the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) after him is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. And Abu Dawud also narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah: He said: I said, O my father, who is the best of people after the Messenger of Allah? He said: Abu Bakr. I said: Then who? He said: Umar. He said: Then I feared that if I said: Then who, he would say: Uthman, so I said: You, O my father? He said: I am but a man from the Muslims. So by these hadiths is established what we claimed of the order of virtue, and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument for the superiority of Uthman ibn Affan over Ali ibn Abi Talib, as held by the Hanafi-Maturidi school. The author begins by stating the school's position: the apparent doctrine is to prefer Uthman after Abu Bakr and Umar. He then notes dissenting views: al-Husayn al-Bajali and Ibn Khuzaymah preferred Ali over Uthman, while al-Qalanisi suspended judgment but held that an inferior can be imam. Next, the author defends Uthman's merits: his financial support of Islam, the Prophet's high regard for him at the pledge of Ridwan, and the report that angels feel shy of him. He then addresses a potential objection—Uthman's withdrawal at the Battle of Uhud might suggest cowardice. He refutes this by explaining that even a brave hero may retreat due to hidden reasons, and that true bravery is recklessness with death, which Uthman later displayed during his siege (the 'day of the house') when he prevented his supporters from fighting and offered to free slaves if they laid down arms. This, the author argues, shows his courage. He also cites the conquests during Uthman's caliphate as a greater good than personal battlefield heroism. Finally, he provides hadith evidence from Abu Dawud: Ibn Umar reports that during the Prophet's time, the Companions considered the order of virtue as Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. Another hadith from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah implicitly affirms this order. The author concludes that these hadiths establish the claimed order of virtue. The lesson thus combines rational argument and transmitted evidence to defend the Hanafi position on the ranking of the first three caliphs.

Key Terms
تفضيل
فضيلة
خلفاء راشدين
بيعة الرضوان
صحيح أبي داود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi school prefers Ali over Uthman.
Correction: The text shows that the Hanafi-Maturidi school holds that Uthman is preferred after Abu Bakr and Umar, placing him above Ali.

Checkpoint
What is the apparent Hanafi position on the ranking of the caliphs according to this passage?
A) Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Ali, then Uthman
B) Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali
C) Abu Bakr, then Ali, then Umar, then Uthman
D) Equality among the first four caliphs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, supported by hadith and rational argument, holds that Uthman ibn Affan is the third best of the Companions after Abu Bakr and Umar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the ranking of the Companions, do you ever catch yourself preferring someone based on your own cultural or personal biases rather than the evidence from the Quran and Sunnah? How can you ensure your heart aligns with what Allah and His Messenger have indicated?
?
Uthman's courage was shown in forbidding his supporters to fight and in his patience during the siege. If you were in a situation where your honor or life was threatened, would you have the same level of trust in Allah and restraint, or would you be quick to defend yourself? What would it take to develop that kind of courage?
?
The Prophet placed Uthman's hand as his own at the Pledge of Ridwan, signifying a deep bond. Do you love Uthman and all the Companions with the same reverence? Or do you find that your heart is divided, perhaps influenced by sectarian debates? Examine yourself: do you truly accept the collective merit of the Companions as a whole?

Ali's Virtue and the Scope of the Book
Nasafi affirms Ali's virtue but limits discussion to the order of the four caliphs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's affirmation of Ali's virtue and why he limits his book's discussion to the order of the caliphs.

Core Idea
Nasafi affirms Ali's virtue but keeps focus on the caliphal ranking.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses the topic of Ali's preference. He acknowledges Ali's exceptional virtues but clarifies the limited scope of his book: to establish the order of virtue among the rightly guided caliphs, not to detail each Companion's merits. This helps the reader understand the author's intention and avoid misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow from affirming Ali's virtue to stating the book's purpose.
2
Identify Nasafi's affirmation of Ali
Note that Nasafi says no rational person would deny Ali's superiority during his caliphate due to his unique combination of knowledge, piety, diligence, and bravery.
3
Understand the book's purpose
Nasafi states that his book's aim is to explain the order in virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs, not to detail all Companion virtues.
4
Reflect on the closure
Nasafi says he has already completed that purpose, so further elaboration is unnecessary. He directs readers to earlier works for more details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في تفضيل علي: لا أعلم أحدًا يرجع إلى عقل وعلم يمتنع من تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على جميع أهل زمان خلافته إذ لم يجتمع من أحد منهم ما اجتمع فيه من العلم والورع والاجتهاد في الدين والشجاعة، وليس الغرض من كتابنا هذا بيان فضائل الصحابة رضي الله عنهم لنشتغل بذلك بل كان غرضنا بيان الترتيب في الفضيلة بين الخلفاء الراشدين. وقد فرغنا من ذلك بحمد الله تعالى - فلا معنى للإطالة ببيان فضيلة كل واحد منهم إذ كتب السلف مشحونة بذلك، فمن رام الوقوف عليه فلينظر فيها، والله الهادي للعباد إلى سبيل الرشاد.
Discussion on the preference of Ali: I do not know anyone who relies on intellect and knowledge who would refrain from preferring Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, over all the people of his time of his caliphate, since there was not combined in any one of them what was combined in him of knowledge, piety, striving in religion, and bravery. But the purpose of this book of ours is not to explain the merits of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) so that we occupy ourselves with that; rather our purpose was to explain the order in virtue among the rightly guided caliphs. And we have finished that by the praise of Allah the Exalted — so there is no meaning in prolonging by explaining the virtue of each one of them, for the books of the predecessors are full of that. So whoever aims to find it, let him look into them. And Allah is the guide of servants to the path of rectitude.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a brief doctrinal statement on the virtue of Ali ibn Abi Talib, followed by a methodological note on the scope of the book. The author begins by asserting that no rational and knowledgeable person would deny Ali's superiority over all others during his caliphate, due to his unique combination of knowledge, piety, religious diligence, and bravery. However, the author immediately clarifies that the purpose of this book is not to detail the virtues of all the Companions; rather, its aim was to establish the order of virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali). Since he has already completed that task (in the previous section and the book as a whole), he sees no need to elaborate further on each caliph's individual merits. He directs readers who wish to know more to the earlier scholarly works that are filled with such details. The passage thus serves as a closure to the discussion on the caliphs' ranking and reinforces the book's limited objective.

Key Terms
تفضيل
خلفاء راشدين
فضائل الصحابة
علم
ورع
شجاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author is dismissing Ali's virtue.
Correction: Nasafi affirms Ali's virtue but restricts the discussion to the caliphal order, not dismissing it.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the purpose of the book in discussing the caliphs?
A) To enumerate all the virtues of the Companions
B) To prove that Ali is superior to Uthman
C) To explain the order of virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs
D) To argue that all caliphs are equal in virtue

Key Takeaway
Nasafi affirms Ali's excellence but restricts his book's scope to ranking the caliphs, directing readers to other works for detailed virtues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islam, do you allow yourself to be sidetracked by debates about the virtues of individuals instead of focusing on the core purpose of the lesson? What is the cost of that distraction in your own spiritual growth?
?
Nasafi affirms Ali's excellence but then limits his discourse, saying 'we have finished that'. Do you know when to stop in your own religious discussions, or do you feel compelled to prove every point fully, even when the books are already filled with it? How does that affect your humility?
?
The author directs readers to other books for details. Are you willing to go to primary sources yourself, or do you expect everything to be handed to you in a single lesson? What does that say about your intellectual responsibility in seeking knowledge?

Hadith evidence for the ranking of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman
The author cites two hadith to prove the order of virtue among the first three caliphs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith evidence for the order of virtue among Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman.

Core Idea
The companions themselves affirmed the ranking of Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman.

This passage presents two key hadith that establish the order of virtue among the first three caliphs. The author uses these narrations to support the Sunni position on the ranking of the Companions. The narrations come from authoritative sources and reflect the consensus of the early community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the content and flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the names and the sequence mentioned.
2
Identify the two hadith
Note that the passage contains two separate narrations: one from Ibn Umar and one from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah. Identify the narrators and the context.
The second hadith involves a conversation with Ali ibn Abi Talib.
3
Understand the implication for ranking
Consider how each hadith confirms the order of virtue: the first shows the companions' view during the Prophet's lifetime; the second shows Ali's own acknowledgment of the ranking.
This establishes the order as Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author states that by these hadith, their claim of the order of virtue is established. Reflect on how scriptural evidence is used to support doctrinal positions.
The phrase 'والله الموفق' shows reliance on God for success.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروى أيضا عن ابن عمر أنه قال: كنا نقول ورسول الله عليه السلام حي: أفضل أمة النبي عليه السلام بعده أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان. وروى أبو داود أيضا عن محمد بن الحنفية: قال: قلت يا أبي أي الناس خير بعد رسول الله؟ قال: أبو بكر، قلت: ثم من؟ قال: عمر، قال: ثم خشيت أن أقول: ثم من، فيقول: عثمان، فقلت أنت يا أبت؟ قال: ما أنا إلا رجل من المسلمين. فثبت بهذه الأحاديث ما ادعينا من ترتيب الفضيلة، والله الموفق.
He also narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'We used to say while the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) was alive: The best of the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) after him is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman.' And Abu Dawud also narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah: He said: 'I said: O my father, who is the best of people after the Messenger of God? He said: Abu Bakr. I said: Then who? He said: Umar. He said: Then I was afraid to say: Then who? so he would say: Uthman, so I said: (Are) you, O my father? He said: I am only a man among the Muslims.' So by these hadiths is established what we claimed regarding the order of virtue. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage provides scriptural evidence for the ranking in virtue among the first three caliphs. The author presents two hadith: one from Ibn Umar reporting the consensus of the companions during the Prophet's lifetime, and one from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah asking his father Ali, who confirms the order. The author concludes that these hadith prove the sequence of virtue (Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman).

Key Terms
ترتيب الفضيلة
الخلفاء الراشدون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the ranking of the first three caliphs was invented later by scholars or based on political bias.
Correction: The hadith show that even during the Prophet's lifetime, the companions held this ranking, and Ali himself acknowledged it, confirming its early and authentic basis.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith of Ibn Umar, what is the order of virtue after the Prophet?
A) Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman
B) Umar, then Abu Bakr, then Uthman
C) Ali, then Abu Bakr, then Umar
D) Only Abu Bakr is best

Key Takeaway
The two hadith cited by the author establish the order of virtue among the first three caliphs as Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, based on the testimony of the companions themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The companions unanimously agreed on the ranking of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman while the Prophet was still alive. Do you internally accept this order without any hesitation or preference for another? If you feel any resistance, what is its root, and how will you align your heart with what the companions testified?
?
Ali's humility led him to refrain from praising himself, even when his son asked. When you think about your own status and reputation, do you ever find yourself seeking recognition or feeling slighted when overlooked? How can you cultivate the same self-effacing humility that Ali displayed?
?
These hadiths are clear, authentic proofs from the Prophet's companions. Is there any part of you that doubts or minimizes their authority because of modern narratives? What action will you take to strengthen your conviction in the established virtues of the early Muslims?

Ali's Superiority During His Caliphate
Author argues Ali's superiority over his contemporaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument for Ali's superiority over his contemporaries based on his unique combination of knowledge, piety, diligence, and bravery.

Core Idea
Ali's unmatched qualities make his superiority evident to any rational person.

In this passage, the author makes a bold claim about Ali's status among his contemporaries. He asserts that no rational and knowledgeable person would deny Ali's superiority, because he possessed a unique combination of qualities. This lesson examines that argument and the qualities listed.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about Ali
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the strong language the author uses: 'I know of no one...'.
The phrase 'يرجع إلى عقل وعلم' emphasizes rationality and knowledge.
2
Identify the four qualities
List the four qualities the author attributes to Ali: knowledge (`ilm), piety (wara`), diligence in religion (ijtihad fi al-din), and bravery (shaja`ah). Consider why these are significant.
These qualities are not arbitrary; they cover intellectual, spiritual, and practical domains.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself: Why does the author claim that no one with reason would disagree? What does this tell us about the author's view of Ali's status?
The argument rests on the uniqueness of Ali's combination, not just any one quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا أعلم أحدًا يرجع إلى عقل وعلم يمتنع من تفضيل علي له على جميع أهل زمان خلافته إذ لم يجتمع من أحد منهم ما اجتمع فيه من العلم والورع والاجتهاد في الدين والشجاعة، وليس الغرض من كتابنا هذا بيان فضائل الصحابة رضي الله عنهم لنشتغل بذلك بل كان غرضنا بيان الترتيب في الفضيلة بين الخلفاء الراشدين. وقد فرغنا من ذلك بحمد الله تعالى فلا معنى للإطالة ببيان فضيلة كل واحد منهم إذ كتب السلف مشحونة بذلك، فمن رام الوقوف عليه فلينظر فيها، والله الهادي للعباد إلى سبيل الرشاد.
I know of no one who has recourse to reason and knowledge who refrains from preferring Ali over all the people of his time of his caliphate, since there was not gathered in anyone from among them what was gathered in him of knowledge, piety, diligence in religion, and bravery. And the purpose of our book is not to expound upon the virtues of the Companions (may God be pleased with them) so that we occupy ourselves with that, rather our purpose was to explain the order of virtue among the rightly guided caliphs. And we have completed that, by the praise of God Almighty, so there is no meaning to lengthening by explaining the virtue of each one of them, for the books of the predecessors are filled with that. So whoever desires to come upon it, let him look into them. And God is the guide of the servants to the path of rectitude.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is an argument and clarification. The author asserts that anyone with reason and knowledge would not deny the superiority of Ali over all people during his caliphate, because he possessed the highest degrees of knowledge, piety, religious effort, and bravery. Then the author explains that the book's aim was not to list all the virtues of all companions, but only to establish the order of virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs, which has already been done. He directs those seeking detailed virtues to the books of the predecessors.

Key Terms
تفضيل
العلم
الورع
الاجتهاد
الشجاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the author's claim that Ali is superior to all people during his caliphate?
A) Ali was the only caliph from the Prophet's family
B) Ali combined knowledge, piety, diligence, and bravery, which no one else did
C) Ali was the most beloved companion to the Prophet
D) The author was biased toward Ali

Key Takeaway
Ali's superiority during his caliphate is defended by referencing his unique combination of four essential qualities that no other contemporary possessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own heart: When you hear the claim that Ali's superiority is evident to any rational person, do you find yourself resisting or accepting it? What personal biases or sectarian attachments might prevent you from being that 'rational person'? Are you willing to honestly examine those barriers and submit to the truth regardless of where it leads you?
?
The author lists four qualities: knowledge, piety, diligence in religion, and bravery. Which of these do you truly seek in your own life? If you were to hold yourself to the standard of Ali's example, where do you fall short? What specific action will you take today to grow in even one of these areas?
?
The passage restricts its claim to Ali's caliphate. Could you sincerely affirm, based on the evidence of these four qualities, that Ali was superior to his contemporaries? Or have you been influenced by narratives that pre-decide your loyalties? How would your perspective change if you evaluated each companion solely on the basis of the virtues demonstrated?

The Book's Purpose: Order of Virtue Among the Caliphs
Author clarifies book's limited purpose and directs to other sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's clarification that his book's aim is limited to establishing the ranking of the four rightly guided caliphs, not detailing all companions' virtues.

Core Idea
The book focuses on the order of virtue among the caliphs, not on comprehensive companion biographies.

After asserting Ali's superiority, the author clarifies the scope of his work. He explains that his goal was not to list all the virtues of the Companions, but only to establish the order of virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs, which he claims to have completed. He then directs readers to earlier works for more detailed accounts.

Guided Path
1
Read the purpose statement
Read the Arabic passage where the author states what the book is and is not about. Notice the contrast: 'ليس الغرض... بل كان غرضنا'.
The word 'غرض' (purpose) is key.
2
Identify what has been achieved
The author says 'وقد فرغنا من ذلك' (we have completed that). What is 'that'? Understanding this shows the book's scope.
The completed task is the order of virtue among the caliphs.
3
Understand the recommendation
The author advises readers to look into 'كتب السلف' (books of the predecessors) for detailed virtues. Why does he not expand further?
He avoids redundancy because those books already exist.

Source Text

I know of no one who has recourse to reason and knowledge who refrains from preferring Ali over all the people of his time of his caliphate, since there was not gathered in anyone from among them what was gathered in him of knowledge, piety, diligence in religion, and bravery. And the purpose of our book is not to expound upon the virtues of the Companions (may God be pleased with them) so that we occupy ourselves with that, rather our purpose was to explain the order of virtue among the rightly guided caliphs. So whoever desires to come upon it, let him look into them. And God is the guide of the servants to the path of rectitude.

This passage is an argument and clarification. The author asserts that anyone with reason and knowledge would not deny the superiority of Ali over all people during his caliphate, because he possessed the highest degrees of knowledge, piety, religious effort, and bravery. Then the author explains that the book's aim was not to list all the virtues of all companions, but only to establish the order of virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs, which has already been done. Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the book according to the author?
A) To list all the virtues of the Companions
B) To explain the order of virtue among the four rightly guided caliphs
C) To prove that Ali is superior to all
D) To write a biography of each caliph

Key Takeaway
The book's limited aim is to rank the four caliphs in virtue, and readers are directed to earlier works for detailed companion virtues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author deliberately limited his book to a specific purpose and then stopped, directing readers elsewhere for more. In your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge, are you disciplined enough to focus on your primary objectives, or do you often get sidetracked by tangential details? What will you do to sharpen your focus starting today?
?
When the author says, 'we have completed that', he signals a sense of fulfilled duty. Is there an obligation in your life—whether religious, familial, or communal—that you keep postponing under the guise of 'needing more knowledge'? What would it take for you to be able to say 'I have completed that' with sincerity before Allah?
?
The author humbly refers to earlier works as 'filled' with virtues. Do you have the humility to recognize when your own knowledge is insufficient and to seek out those who are more learned? Or does pride prevent you from consulting others? Make a concrete plan this week to ask a scholar or read a classical text on a topic you know little about.
